</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:1px solid #aaa;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">
<a href="/wiki/File:Socrates.png" class="image"><img alt="Socrates.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" decoding="async" width="10" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" data-file-width="326" data-file-height="500" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Philosophy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Hindu philosophy</b> refers to philosophies,world views and teachings <sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> that emerged in <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">ancient India</a>. These include six systems (<i><a href="/wiki/Dar%C5%9Bana" title="Darśana">ṣaḍdarśana</a></i>) – <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Sankhya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(philosophy)" title="Yoga (philosophy)">Yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaisheshika" title="Vaisheshika">Vaisheshika</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C4%ABm%C4%81%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Mīmāṃsā">Mimamsa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-andrewoverview_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-andrewoverview-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> These are also called the <a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Āstika and nāstika">Astika</a> (orthodox) philosophical traditions and are those that accept the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> as an authoritative, important source of knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-KK26_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KK26-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> Ancient and medieval India was also the source of philosophies that share philosophical concepts but rejected the Vedas, and these have been called <i>nāstika</i> (heterodox or non-orthodox) Indian philosophies.<sup id="cite_ref-andrewoverview_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-andrewoverview-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KK26_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KK26-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Nāstika Indian philosophies include <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/C%C4%81rv%C4%81ka" class="mw-redirect" title="Cārvāka">Cārvāka</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C4%80j%C4%ABvika" title="Ājīvika">Ājīvika</a>, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-bilimoriaastika_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bilimoriaastika-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have debated the relationship and differences within āstika philosophies and with nāstika philosophies, starting with the writings of <a href="/wiki/Indology" title="Indology">Indologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalists</a> of the 18th and 19th centuries, which were themselves derived from limited availability of Indian literature and medieval doxographies.<sup id="cite_ref-andrewoverview_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-andrewoverview-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The various sibling traditions included in Hindu philosophies are diverse, and they are united by shared history and concepts, same textual resources, similar ontological and soteriological focus, and cosmology.<sup id="cite_ref-frazierintrop2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frazierintrop2-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> While Buddhism and Jainism are considered distinct philosophies and religions, some heterodox traditions such as Cārvāka are often considered as distinct schools within Hindu philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KN_Tiwari_1998_page_67_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KN_Tiwari_1998_page_67-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindu philosophy also includes several sub-schools of theistic philosophies that integrate ideas from two or more of the six orthodox philosophies, such as the realism of the Nyāya, the naturalism of the Vaiśeṣika, the dualism of the Sāṅkhya, the <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a> and knowledge of Self as essential to liberation of Advaita, the self-discipline of yoga and the asceticism and elements of theistic ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-klaussaiva_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klaussaiva-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996132-136,_162-169,_231-232_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996132-136,_162-169,_231-232-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Examples of such schools include <a href="/wiki/Pashupata_Shaivism" title="Pashupata Shaivism">Pāśupata Śaiva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaiva_Siddhanta" title="Shaiva Siddhanta">Śaiva siddhānta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pratyabhijna" title="Pratyabhijna">Pratyabhijña</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rase%C5%9Bvara" title="Raseśvara">Raseśvara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaiṣṇava</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-klaussaiva_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klaussaiva-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996132-136,_162-169,_231-232_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996132-136,_162-169,_231-232-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Some sub-schools share Tantric ideas with those found in some Buddhist traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Klaus_K._Klostermaier_1984_pages_219-223_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klaus_K._Klostermaier_1984_pages_219-223-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The ideas of these sub-schools are found in the <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%80gama_(Hinduism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Āgama (Hinduism)">Āgamas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Each school of Hindu philosophy has extensive <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemological</a> literature called <i><a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramāṇaśāstras</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter1991172_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter1991172-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuttorm_Fløistad1993137-154_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuttorm_Fløistad1993137-154-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> as well as theories on <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Axiology" title="Axiology">axiology</a>, and other topics.<sup id="cite_ref-karlpotterov_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karlpotterov-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifications">Classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Āstika and nāstika">Āstika and nāstika</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hinduism" title="History of Hinduism">history of Hinduism</a>, the six orthodox schools had emerged before the start of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Some scholars have questioned whether the orthodox and heterodox schools classification is sufficient or accurate, given the diversity and evolution of views within each major school of Hindu philosophy, with some sub-schools combining heterodox and orthodox views.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter199198-102_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter199198-102-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since ancient times Indian philosophy has been categorized into <i>āstika</i> and <i>nāstika</i> schools of thought.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson2010_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson2010-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The orthodox schools of Hindu philosophy have been called <b>ṣaḍdarśana</b> ("six systems"). This schema was created between the 12th and 16th centuries by <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedantins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-uh14_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uh14-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2–3</span></sup> It was then adopted by the early Western <a href="/wiki/Indology" title="Indology">Indologists</a>, and pervades modern understandings of Hindu philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-uh14_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uh14-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4–5</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".C4.80stika"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Āstika">Āstika</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Āstika">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are six āstika (orthodox) schools of thought.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> Each is called a <a href="/wiki/Dar%C5%9Bana" title="Darśana">darśana</a>, and each darśana accepts the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> as authoritative and the premise that <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">ātman</a></i> (soul, eternal self) exists.<sup id="cite_ref-KK26_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KK26-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">āstika</i> schools are:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a>, an atheistic and strongly dualist theoretical exposition of <a href="/wiki/Purusha" title="Purusha">consciousness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prak%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Prakṛti">matter</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yoga_(philosophy)" title="Yoga (philosophy)">Yoga</a>, a school emphasising <a href="/wiki/Dhyana_in_Hinduism" title="Dhyana in Hinduism">meditation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">contemplation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaivalya" title="Kaivalya">liberation</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyāya</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indian_logic" title="Indian logic">logic</a>, which explores <a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">sources of knowledge</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Ny%C4%81ya_S%C5%ABtras" title="Nyāya Sūtras">Nyāya Sūtras</a></i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vai%C5%9Be%E1%B9%A3ika" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaiśeṣika">Vaiśeṣika</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricist</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/M%C4%ABm%C4%81%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Mīmāṃsā">Mīmāṃsā</a>, an anti-ascetic and anti-mysticist school of <a href="/wiki/Orthopraxy" title="Orthopraxy">orthopraxy</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedānta</a>, the last segment of knowledge in the Vedas, or <i>jñānakāṇḍa</i>. Vedānta came to be the dominant current of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> in the post-medieval period.</li></ol>
<h3><span id="N.C4.81stika"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nāstika">Nāstika</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nāstika">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jain_philosophy" title="Jain philosophy">Jain philosophy</a></div>
<p>Schools that do not accept the authority of the Vedas are nāstika philosophies, of which four <i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">nāstika</i> (heterodox) schools are prominent:<sup id="cite_ref-bilimoriaastika_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bilimoriaastika-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/C%C4%81rv%C4%81ka" class="mw-redirect" title="Cārvāka">Cārvāka</a>, a materialism school that accepted the existence of free will.<sup id="cite_ref-bhattacarv_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhattacarv-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bronkhorst_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bronkhorst-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/%C4%80j%C4%ABvika" title="Ājīvika">Ājīvika</a>, a materialism school that denied the existence of free will.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, a philosophy that denies existence of <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Buddhism)" title="Ātman (Buddhism)">ātman</a> (soul, self)<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and is based on the teachings and enlightenment of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, a philosophy that accepts the existence of the ātman (soul, self), and is based on the teachings and enlightenment of twenty-four teachers known as <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">tirthankaras</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Rishabha" class="mw-redirect" title="Rishabha">Rishabha</a> as the first and <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a> as the twenty-fourth.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_schools">Other schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Other schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Besides the major orthodox and non-orthodox schools, there have existed syncretic sub-schools that have combined ideas and introduced new ones of their own. The medieval scholar <a href="/wiki/Madhvacharya" title="Madhvacharya">Madhva Acharya</a> (CE 1238–1317) includes the following, along with Buddhism<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and Jainism,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> as sub-schools of Hindu philosophy:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Pashupata_Shaivism" title="Pashupata Shaivism">Pashupata Shaivism</a>, developed by Nakulisa<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shaiva_Siddhanta" title="Shaiva Siddhanta">Shaiva Siddhanta</a>, the theistic Sankhya school<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing200745_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing200745-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pratyabhijna" title="Pratyabhijna">Pratyabhijña</a>, the recognitive school of <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_Shaivism" title="Kashmir Shaivism">Kashmir Shaivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trika" class="mw-redirect" title="Trika">Trika</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rase%C5%9Bvara" title="Raseśvara">Raseśvara</a>, a Shaiva school that advocated the use of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> to reach immortality<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ramanuja" title="Ramanuja">Ramanuja</a> school<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Pūrṇaprājña (<a href="/wiki/Madhvacharya" title="Madhvacharya">Madhvācārya</a>) school<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇinīya</a><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The above sub-schools introduced their own ideas while adopting concepts from orthodox schools of Hindu philosophy such as realism of the Nyāya, naturalism of Vaiśeṣika, monism and knowledge of Self (Atman) as essential to liberation of Advaita, self-discipline of Yoga, asceticism and elements of theistic ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-klaussaiva_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klaussaiva-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Some sub-schools share Tantric ideas with those found in some Buddhist traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Klaus_K._Klostermaier_1984_pages_219-223_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klaus_K._Klostermaier_1984_pages_219-223-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">Pramana</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramāṇa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002520-521_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002520-521-53">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> It has been a key, much debated field of study in Hinduism since ancient times. Pramāṇa is a Hindu theory of knowledge and discusses means by which human beings gain accurate knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002520-521_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELochtefeld2002520-521-53">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The focus of pramāṇa is how correct knowledge can be acquired, how one knows, how one doesn't, and to what extent knowledge pertinent about someone or something can be acquired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPotter1991172_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPotter1991172-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient and medieval Hindu texts identify six pramāṇas as correct means of accurate knowledge and truths: <i>pratyakṣa</i> (perception), <i>anumāṇa</i> (inference), <i>upamāṇa</i> (comparison and analogy), <i>arthāpatti</i> (postulation, derivation from circumstances), <i>anupalabdi</i> (non-perception, negative/cognitive proof) and <i>śabda</i> (word, testimony of past or present reliable experts)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Each of these are further categorized in terms of conditionality, completeness, confidence and possibility of error, by the different schools. The schools vary on how many of these six are valid paths of knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuttorm_Fløistad1993137-154_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuttorm_Fløistad1993137-154-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Cārvāka</a> nāstika philosophy holds that only one (perception) is an epistemically reliable means of knowledge,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> school holds that three are (perception, inference and testimony),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> while the Mīmāṃsā and Advaita schools hold that all six are epistemically useful and reliable means to knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="S.C4.81mkhya"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sāmkhya">Sāmkhya</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sāmkhya">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a></div>
<p>Samkhya is the oldest of the orthodox philosophical systems in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996232_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996232-57">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> with origins in the 1st millennium BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Mike_Burley_2012_pages_43-46_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mike_Burley_2012_pages_43-46-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and had a strong influence on other schools of Indian philosophies.<sup id="cite_ref-royper_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royper-59">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Sāmkhya is an enumerationist philosophy whose <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> accepted three of six <i><a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">pramāṇas</a></i> as the only reliable means of gaining knowledge. These were <i>pratyakṣa</i> (perception), <i>anumāṇa</i> (inference) and <i>sabda</i> (<i>Āptavacana</i>, word/testimony of reliable sources).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Samkhya school espouses <a href="/wiki/Dualism_(Indian_philosophy)" title="Dualism (Indian philosophy)">dualism</a> between consciousness and matter.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> It regards the universe as consisting of two realities: <i><a href="/wiki/Purusha" title="Purusha">Puruṣa</a></i> (consciousness) and <i><a href="/wiki/Prakriti" class="mw-redirect" title="Prakriti">prakriti</a></i> (matter). <i><a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">Jiva</a></i> (a living being) is that state in which <i>puruṣa</i> is bonded to <i>prakriti</i> in some form.<sup id="cite_ref-sambri_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sambri-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> This fusion, state the Samkhya scholars, led to the emergence of <i>buddhi</i> (awareness, intellect) and <i>ahankara</i> (individualized ego consciousness, “I-maker”). The universe is described by this school as one created by Purusa-Prakriti entities infused with various permutations and combinations of variously enumerated elements, senses, feelings, activity and mind.<sup id="cite_ref-sambri_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sambri-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Samkhya philosophy includes a theory of <a href="/wiki/Gu%E1%B9%87a" title="Guṇa">gunas</a> (qualities, innate tendencies, psyche).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Guna, it states, are of three types: <i>Sattva</i> being good, compassionate, illuminating, positive, and constructive; <i>Rajas</i> guna is one of activity, chaotic, passion, impulsive, potentially good or bad; and <i>Tamas</i> being the quality of darkness, ignorance, destructive, lethargic, negative. Everything, all life forms and human beings, state Samkhya scholars, have these three <i>gunas</i>, but in different proportions.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The interplay of these <i>gunas</i> defines the character of someone or something, of nature and determines the progress of life.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haney_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haney-66">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Samkhya theorises a pluralism of souls (<i>Jeevatmas</i>) who possess consciousness, but denies the existence of <a href="/wiki/Ishvara" title="Ishvara">Ishvara</a> (God).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Classical Samkhya is considered an atheist or non-theistic Hindu philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Samkhyakarika" title="Samkhyakarika">Samkhya karika</a></i>, one of the key texts of this school of Hindu philosophy, opens by stating its goal to be "three<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> kinds of human suffering" and means to prevent them.<sup id="cite_ref-colebrookesktrans_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colebrookesktrans-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The text then presents a distillation of its theories on epistemology, metaphysics, axiology and soteriology. For example, it states,
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>From the triad of suffering, arises this inquiry into the means of preventing it.<br />That is useless - if you say so, I say: No, because suffering is not absolute and final. – Verse 1<br /><br />
The <a href="/wiki/Gu%E1%B9%87a" title="Guṇa">Guṇas</a> (qualities) respectively consist in pleasure, pain and dullness, are adapted to manifestation, activity and restraint; mutually domineer, rest on each other, produce each other, consort together, and are reciprocally present. – Verse 12<br />
Goodness is considered to be alleviating and enlightening; foulness, urgent and persisting; darkness, heavy and enveloping. Like a lamp, they cooperate for a purpose by union of contraries. – Verse 13<br /><br />
There is a general cause, which is diffuse. It operates by means of the three qualities, by mixture, by modification; for different objects are diversified by influence of the several qualities respectively. – Verse 16<br />
Since the assemblage of perceivable objects is for use (by man); Since the converse of that which has the three qualities with other properties must exist (in man); Since there must be superintendence (within man); Since there must be some entity that enjoys (within man); Since there is a tendency to abstraction (in man), therefore soul is. – Verse 17
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Samkhya karika, <sup id="cite_ref-colebrookesktrans_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colebrookesktrans-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The soteriology in Samkhya aims at the realization of Puruṣa as distinct from Prakriti; this knowledge of the <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Self</a> is held to end transmigration and lead to absolute freedom (<a href="/wiki/Kaivalya" title="Kaivalya">kaivalya</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Yoga">Yoga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Yoga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(philosophy)" title="Yoga (philosophy)">Yoga (philosophy)</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophy</a>, Yoga is, among other things, the name of one of the six āstika philosophical schools.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(philosophy)" title="Yoga (philosophy)">Yoga</a> philosophical system aligns closely with the <a href="/wiki/Dualism_(Indian_philosophy)" title="Dualism (Indian philosophy)">dualist</a> premises of the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> school.<sup id="cite_ref-edbryant_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edbryant-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The Yoga school accepts Samkhya psychology and metaphysics, but is considered theistic because it accepts the concept of personal god (<a href="/wiki/Ishvara" title="Ishvara">Ishvara</a>), unlike Samkhya.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> of the Yoga school, like the Sāmkhya school, relies on three of six prāmaṇas as the means of gaining reliable knowledge:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <i>pratyakṣa</i> (perception), <i>anumāṇa</i> (inference) and <i>śabda</i> (<i>āptavacana</i>, word/testimony of reliable sources).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The universe is conceptualized as a duality in Yoga school: <a href="/wiki/Purusha" title="Purusha">puruṣa</a> (consciousness) and <a href="/wiki/Prak%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Prakṛti">prakṛti</a> (matter); however, the Yoga school discusses this concept more generically as "seer, experiencer" and "seen, experienced" than the Samkhya school.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A key text of the Yoga school is the <i><a href="/wiki/Yoga_Sutras_of_Patanjali" title="Yoga Sutras of Patanjali">Yoga Sutras of Patanjali</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Patanjali" title="Patanjali">Patanjali</a> may have been, as <a href="/wiki/Max_M%C3%BCller" title="Max Müller">Max Müller</a> explains, "the author or representative of the Yoga-philosophy without being necessarily the author of the Sutras."<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Hindu philosophy recognizes many types of Yoga, such as <a href="/wiki/R%C4%81ja_yoga" title="Rāja yoga">rāja yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jnana_yoga" title="Jnana yoga">jñāna yoga</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karma_yoga" title="Karma yoga">karma yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_yoga" title="Bhakti yoga">bhakti yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">tantra</a> yoga, <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a> yoga, <a href="/wiki/Kundalini_yoga" title="Kundalini yoga">laya yoga</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hatha_yoga" title="Hatha yoga">hatha yoga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Yoga school builds on the Samkhya school theory that <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B1%C4%81na" class="mw-redirect" title="Jñāna">jñāna</a> (knowledge) is a sufficient means to moksha. It suggests that systematic techniques/practice (personal experimentation) combined with Samkhya's approach to knowledge is the path to moksha.<sup id="cite_ref-edbryant_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edbryant-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Yoga shares several central ideas with Advaita Vedanta, with the difference that Yoga is a form of experimental mysticism while Advaita Vedanta is a form of <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monistic</a> personalism.<sup id="cite_ref-shphillips_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shphillips-85">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Like Advaita Vedanta, the Yoga school of Hindu philosophy holds that liberation/freedom in this life is achievable, and that this occurs when an individual fully understands and realizes the equivalence of Atman (soul, self) and Brahman.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Vai.C5.9Be.E1.B9.A3ika"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaiśeṣika">Vaiśeṣika</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Vaiśeṣika">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vaisheshika" title="Vaisheshika">Vaisheshika</a></div>
<p>The Vaiśeṣika philosophy is a <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalist</a> school.<sup id="cite_ref-Dale_Riepe_1996_pages_227-246_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dale_Riepe_1996_pages_227-246-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> It is a form of <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a> in natural philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-jganeri_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jganeri-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> It postulates that all objects in the physical universe are reducible to <i>paramāṇu</i> (<a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>), and that one's experiences are derived from the interplay of substance (a function of atoms, their number and their spatial arrangements), quality, activity, commonness, particularity and inherence.<sup id="cite_ref-olilv_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilv-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Knowledge and liberation are achievable by complete understanding of the world of experience, according to Vaiśeṣika school.<sup id="cite_ref-olilv_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilv-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The Vaiśeṣika darśana is credited to <a href="/wiki/Kanada_(philosopher)" title="Kanada (philosopher)">Kaṇāda Kaśyapa</a> from the second half of the first millennium BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-olilv_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilv-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brannigan_2009_page_7_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brannigan_2009_page_7-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The foundational text, the <i><a href="/wiki/Vai%C5%9Be%E1%B9%A3ika_S%C5%ABtra" title="Vaiśeṣika Sūtra">Vaiśeṣika Sūtra</a></i>, opens as follows:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Dharma is that from which results the accomplishment of Exaltation and of the Supreme Good. The authoritativeness of the Veda arises from its being an exposition of <i>dharma</i>. The Supreme Good results from knowledge, produced from a particular <i>dharma</i>, of the essence of the Predicables, Substance, Attribute, Action, Genus, Species and Combination, by means of their resemblances and differences.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Vaiśeṣika Sūtra 1.1.1-1.1.4, <sup id="cite_ref-johannesvs_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johannesvs-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The Vaiśeṣika school is related to the Nyāya school but features differences in its <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, metaphysics and ontology.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The epistemology of the Vaiśeṣika school, like <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, accepted only two means to knowledge as reliable – perception and inference.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989225_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989225-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The Vaiśeṣika school and Buddhism both consider their respective scriptures as indisputable and valid means to knowledge, the difference being that the scriptures held to be a valid and reliable source by Vaiśeṣikas were the Vedas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bilimoriaved_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bilimoriaved-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Vaiśeṣika metaphysical premises are founded on a form of atomism, that reality is composed of four substances (earth, water, air, and fire). Each of these four are of two types:<sup id="cite_ref-jganeri_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jganeri-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> atomic (paramāṇu) and composite. An atom is, according to Vaiśeṣika scholars, that which is indestructible (anitya), indivisible, and has a special kind of dimension, called “small” (aṇu). A composite, in this philosophy, is defined to be anything which is divisible into atoms. Whatever human beings perceive is composite, while atoms are invisible.<sup id="cite_ref-jganeri_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jganeri-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The Vaiśeṣikas stated that size, form, truths and everything that human beings experience as a whole is a function of atoms, their number and their spatial arrangements, their <a href="/wiki/Gu%E1%B9%87a" title="Guṇa">guṇa</a> (quality), <i>karma</i> (activity), <i>sāmānya</i> (commonness), <i>viśeṣa</i> (particularity) and <i>amavāya</i> (inherence, inseparable connectedness of everything).<sup id="cite_ref-olilv_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilv-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Ny.C4.81ya"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nyāya">Nyāya</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Nyāya">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ny%C4%81ya" class="mw-redirect" title="Nyāya">Nyāya</a></div>
<p>The Nyāya school is a <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">realist</a> āstika philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-nyabri_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyabri-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996221-227_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996221-227-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The school's most significant contributions to Indian philosophy were its systematic development of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, methodology, and its treatises on epistemology.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The foundational text of the Nyāya school is the <i><a href="/wiki/Ny%C4%81ya_S%C5%ABtras" title="Nyāya Sūtras">Nyāya Sūtras</a></i> of the first millennium BCE. The text is credited to Aksapada Gautama and its composition is variously dated between the sixth and second centuries BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brannigan_2009_page_7_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brannigan_2009_page_7-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nyāya epistemology accepts four out of six <i>prāmaṇas</i> as reliable means of gaining knowledge – <i>pratyakṣa</i> (perception), <i>anumāṇa</i> (inference), <i>upamāṇa</i> (comparison and analogy) and <i>śabda</i> (word, testimony of past or present reliable experts).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dpsb_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dpsb-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In its <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, the Nyāya school is closer to the Vaiśeṣika school than the others.<sup id="cite_ref-nyabri_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyabri-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> It holds that human suffering results from mistakes/defects produced by activity under wrong knowledge (notions and ignorance).<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Moksha (liberation), it states, is gained through right knowledge. This premise led Nyāya to concern itself with epistemology, that is, the reliable means to gain correct knowledge and to remove wrong notions. False knowledge is not merely ignorance to Naiyayikas; it includes delusion. Correct knowledge is discovering and overcoming one's delusions, and understanding the true nature of the soul, self and reality.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The Nyāya Sūtras begin:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>Perception, Inference, Comparison and Word – these are the means of right knowledge.<br />
Perception is that knowledge which arises from the contact of a sense with its object and which is determinate, unnameable and non-erratic.<br />
Inference is knowledge which is preceded by perception, and is of three kinds: a priori, a posteriori, and commonly seen.<br />
Comparison is the knowledge of a thing through its similarity to another thing previously well known.<br />
Word is the instructive assertion of a reliable person.<br />
It [knowledge] is of two kinds: that which is seen, and that which is not seen.<br />
Soul, body, senses, objects of senses, intellect, mind, activity, fault, transmigration, fruit, suffering and release – are the objects of right knowledge.
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Nyāya Sūtras 1.1.3-1.1.9, <sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span id="M.C4.ABm.C4.81.E1.B9.83s.C4.81"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mīmāṃsā">Mīmāṃsā</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mīmāṃsā">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/M%C4%ABm%C4%81%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Mīmāṃsā">Mīmāṃsā</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/M%C4%ABm%C4%81%E1%B9%83s%C4%81" title="Mīmāṃsā">Mīmāṃsā</a> school emphasized <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-olilm_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilm-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> It is a form of <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">philosophical realism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Key texts of the Mīmāṃsā school are the <i><a href="/wiki/Purva_Mimamsa_Sutras" title="Purva Mimamsa Sutras">Purva Mimamsa Sutras</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Jaimini" title="Jaimini">Jaimini</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-olilpm_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilpm-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mhiri_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mhiri-111">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The classical Mīmāṃsā school is sometimes referred to as <i>pūrvamīmāṃsā</i> or <i>Karmamīmāṃsā</i> in reference to the first part of the Vedas.<sup id="cite_ref-olilpm_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilpm-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mīmāṃsā school has several sub-schools defined by epistemology. The <a href="/wiki/Prabh%C4%81kara" title="Prabhākara">Prābhākara</a> subschool of Mīmāṃsā accepted five means to gaining knowledge as epistimetically reliable: <i>pratyakṣa</i> (perception), <i>anumāṇa</i> (inference), <i>upamāṇa</i> (comparison and analogy), <i>arthāpatti</i> (postulation, derivation from circumstances), and <i>śabda</i> (word, testimony of past or present reliable experts).<sup id="cite_ref-dpsb_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dpsb-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kum%C4%81rila_Bha%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADa" title="Kumārila Bhaṭṭa">Kumārila Bhaṭṭa</a> sub-school of Mīmāṃsā added a sixth way of knowing to its canon of reliable epistemology: <i>anupalabdi</i> (non-perception, negative/cognitive proof).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The metaphysics of the Mīmāṃsā school consists of both <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theistic</a> doctrines, and the school showed little interest in systematic examination of the existence of God. Rather, it held that the soul is an eternal, omnipresent, inherently active spiritual essence, then focussed on the epistemology and metaphysics of <i>dharma</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-olilpm_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilpm-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> To them, <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a> meant rituals and duties, not <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Hinduism)" title="Deva (Hinduism)">devas</a> (gods), because devas existed only in name.<sup id="cite_ref-olilpm_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilpm-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The Mīmāṃsākas held that the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> are "eternal authorless infallible", that Vedic <i>vidhi</i> (injunctions) and <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a> in rituals are prescriptive <i>karya</i> (actions), and that the rituals are of primary importance and merit. They considered the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> and other texts related to self-knowledge and spirituality to be of secondary importance, a philosophical view that the <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> school disagreed with.<sup id="cite_ref-olilm_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilm-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-olilpm_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilpm-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mīmāṃsā gave rise to the study of <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> While their deep analysis of language and <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a> influenced other schools,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> their views were not shared by others. Mīmāṃsākas considered the purpose and power of language was to clearly <i>prescribe</i> the proper, correct and right. In contrast, Vedantins extended the scope and value of language as a tool to also <i>describe</i>, <i>develop</i> and <i>derive</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-olilpm_110-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilpm-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Mīmāṃsākas considered orderly, law-driven, procedural life as the central purpose and noblest necessity of <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a> and society, and divine (theistic) sustenance means to that end. The Mimamsa school was influential and foundational to the Vedanta school, with the difference that Mīmāṃsā developed and emphasized <i>karmakāṇḍa</i> (the portion of the <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aruti" title="Śruti">śruti</a> which relates to ceremonial acts and sacrificial rites, the early parts of the Vedas), while the Vedanta school developed and emphasized <i>jñānakāṇḍa</i> (the portion of the Vedas which relates to knowledge of monism, the latter parts of the Vedas).<sup id="cite_ref-olilm_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olilm-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Ved.C4.81nta"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vedānta">Vedānta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vedānta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedānta</a> school built upon the teachings of the Upanishads and <a href="/wiki/Brahma_Sutras" title="Brahma Sutras">Brahma Sutras</a> from the first millennium BCE<sup id="cite_ref-Michael_Brannigan_2009_page_7_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Brannigan_2009_page_7-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> and is the most developed and best-known of the Hindu schools. The epistemology of the Vedantins included, depending on the sub-school, five or six methods as proper and reliable means of gaining any form of knowledge:<sup id="cite_ref-bilimoriaved_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bilimoriaved-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> <i>pratyakṣa</i> (perception), <i>anumāṇa</i> (inference), <i>upamāṇa</i> (comparison and analogy), <i>arthāpatti</i> (postulation, derivation from circumstances), <i>anupalabdi</i> (non-perception, negative/cognitive proof) and <i>śabda</i> (word, testimony of past or present reliable experts).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerrett2000245-248-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238_55-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes1989238-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996225-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> All of these have been further categorized by each sub-school of Vedanta in terms of conditionality, completeness, confidence and possibility of error.<sup id="cite_ref-bilimoriaved_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bilimoriaved-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The emergence of Vedanta school represented a period when a more knowledge-centered understanding began to emerge. These focussed on <i>jnana</i> (knowledge) driven aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Vedic religion</a> and the Upanishads. This included metaphysical concepts such as ātman and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>, and an emphasis on meditation, self-discipline, self-knowledge and abstract spirituality, rather than ritualism. The Upanishads were variously interpreted by ancient- and medieval-era Vedanta scholars. Consequently, the Vedanta separated into many sub-schools, ranging from theistic dualism to non-theistic monism, each interpreting the texts in its own way and producing its own series of sub-commentaries.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advaita">Advaita</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Advaita">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a></div>
<p><i>Advaita</i> literally means "not two, sole, unity". It is a sub-school of Vedanta, and asserts spiritual and universal non-dualism.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Its metaphysics is a form of absolute <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a>, that is all ultimate reality is interconnected oneness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996239-244_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996239-244-121">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> This is the oldest and most widely acknowledged Vedantic school. The foundational texts of this school are the <a href="/wiki/Brahma_Sutras" title="Brahma Sutras">Brahma Sutras</a> and the early <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> from the 1st millennium BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996239-244_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996239-244-121">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Its first great consolidator was the 8th century scholar <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Adi Shankara</a>, who continued the line of thought of the Upanishadic teachers, and that of his teacher's teacher <a href="/wiki/Gaudapada" title="Gaudapada">Gaudapada</a>. He wrote extensive commentaries on the major Vedantic scriptures and is celebrated as one of the major Hindu philosophers from whose doctrines the main currents of modern Indian thought are derived.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to this school of Vedanta, all reality is Brahman, and there exists nothing whatsoever which is not <i>Brahman</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Its metaphysics includes the concept of <a href="/wiki/Maya_(illusion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Maya (illusion)">māyā</a> and ātman. Māyā connotes "that which exists, but is constantly changing and thus is spiritually unreal".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The empirical reality is considered as always changing and therefore "transitory, incomplete, misleading and not what it appears to be".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The concept of ātman is of soul, self within each person, each living being. Advaita Vedantins assert that ātman is same as Brahman, and this Brahman is within each human being and all life, all living beings are spiritually interconnected, and there is oneness in all of existence.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sharmaadv_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sharmaadv-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> They hold that dualities and misunderstanding of <i>māyā</i> as the spiritual reality that matters is caused by ignorance, and are the cause of sorrow, suffering. <a href="/wiki/Jivanmukta" title="Jivanmukta">Jīvanmukti</a> (liberation during life) can be achieved through Self-knowledge, the understanding that ātman within is same as ātman in another person and all of Brahman – the eternal, unchanging, entirety of cosmic principles and true reality.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sharmaadv_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sharmaadv-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Vi.C5.9Bi.E1.B9.A3.E1.B9.AD.C4.81dvaita"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Viśiṣṭādvaita">Viśiṣṭādvaita</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Viśiṣṭādvaita">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vishishtadvaita" title="Vishishtadvaita">Vishishtadvaita</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ramanuja" title="Ramanuja">Ramanuja</a> (c. 1037–1137) was the foremost proponent of the philosophy of Viśiṣṭādvaita or qualified non-dualism. Viśiṣṭādvaita advocated the concept of a Supreme Being with essential qualities or attributes. Viśiṣṭādvaitins argued against the Advaitin conception of Brahman as an impersonal empty oneness. They saw Brahman as an eternal oneness, but also as the source of all creation, which was omnipresent and actively involved in existence. To them the sense of subject-object perception was illusory and a sign of ignorance. However, the individual's sense of self was not a complete illusion since it was derived from the universal beingness that is Brahman.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Ramanuja saw <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> as a personification of Brahman.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dvaita">Dvaita</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Dvaita">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dvaita" class="mw-redirect" title="Dvaita">Dvaita</a> refers to a theistic sub-school in <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> tradition of Hindu philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-Fowler2002p342_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler2002p342-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Also called as <i>Tattvavāda</i> and <i>Bimbapratibimbavāda</i>, the Dvaita sub-school was founded by the 13th-century scholar <a href="/wiki/Madhvacharya" title="Madhvacharya">Madhvacharya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fowler2002p342_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler2002p342-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The Dvaita Vedanta school believes that <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> (Vishnu, supreme soul) and the individual souls (<a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">jīvātman</a>) exist as independent realities, and these are distinct.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeaneane_D._Fowler_2002_238–243,_288–293,_340–343_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeaneane_D._Fowler_2002_238–243,_288–293,_340–343-135">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lochtefelddvaita_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lochtefelddvaita-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dvaita Vedanta is a dualistic interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a>, espouses dualism by theorizing the existence of two separate realities.<sup id="cite_ref-Fowler2002p342_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler2002p342-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The first and the only independent reality, states the Dvaita school, is that of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> or Brahman.<sup id="cite_ref-Fowler2002p342_133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler2002p342-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Vishnu is the supreme <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">Self</a>, in a manner similar to monotheistic God in other major religions.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The distinguishing factor of Dvaita philosophy, as opposed to monistic <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a>, is that God takes on a personal role and is seen as a real eternal entity that governs and controls the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Like <a href="/wiki/Vishishtadvaita" title="Vishishtadvaita">Vishishtadvaita</a> Vedanta subschool, Dvaita philosophy also embraced <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>, with the metaphysical concept of Brahman in the Vedas identified with Vishnu and the one and only <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Being" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Being">Supreme Being</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stokeriep_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stokeriep-140">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> However, unlike Vishishtadvaita which envisions ultimate qualified nondualism, the dualism of Dvaita was permanent.<sup id="cite_ref-lochtefelddvaita_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lochtefelddvaita-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jeaneane_D._Fowler_2002_238–243,_288–293,_340–343_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeaneane_D._Fowler_2002_238–243,_288–293,_340–343-135">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">Salvation</a>, in Dvaita, is achievable only through the grace of God Vishnu.<sup id="cite_ref-Fowler2002p342_133-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler2002p342-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Dvait.C4.81dvaita_.28Bhedabheda.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dvaitādvaita_(Bhedabheda)">Dvaitādvaita (Bhedabheda)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dvaitādvaita (Bhedabheda)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dvaitadvaita" class="mw-redirect" title="Dvaitadvaita">Dvaitādvaita</a> was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Nimbarka" class="mw-redirect" title="Nimbarka">Nimbarka</a>, a 13th-century Vaishnava Philosopher from the Andhra region. According to this philosophy there are three categories of existence: Brahman, soul, and matter. Soul and matter are different from Brahman in that they have attributes and capacities different from Brahman. Brahman exists independently, while soul and matter are dependent. Thus soul and matter have an existence that is separate yet dependent. Further, Brahman is a controller, the soul is the enjoyer, and matter the thing enjoyed. Also, the highest object of worship is <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> and his consort <a href="/wiki/Radha" title="Radha">Radha</a>, attended by thousands of <i><a href="/wiki/Gopi" title="Gopi">gopis</a></i>; of the <a href="/wiki/Vrindavan" title="Vrindavan">Vrindavan</a>; and devotion consists in self-surrender.
</p>
<h3><span id=".C5.9Auddh.C4.81dvaita"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Śuddhādvaita">Śuddhādvaita</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Śuddhādvaita">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shuddhadvaita" title="Shuddhadvaita">Śuddhādvaita</a> is the "purely non-dual" philosophy propounded by <a href="/wiki/Vallabha_Acharya" class="mw-redirect" title="Vallabha Acharya">Vallabha Acharya</a> (1479–1531). The founding philosopher was also the guru of the Vallabhā <a href="/wiki/Sampradaya" title="Sampradaya">sampradāya</a> ("tradition of Vallabh") or <a href="/wiki/Pushtimarg" title="Pushtimarg"><i>Puṣṭimārga</i></a>, a Vaishnava tradition focused on the worship of Krishna. <a href="/wiki/Vallabhacharya" class="mw-redirect" title="Vallabhacharya">Vallabhacharya</a> enunciates that Brahman has created the world without connection with any external agency such as Māyā (which itself is His power) and manifests Himself through the world.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> That is why <a href="/wiki/Shuddhadvaita" title="Shuddhadvaita">Shuddhadvaita</a> is known as ‘Unmodified transformation’ or ‘Avikṛta Pariṇāmavāda’. Brahman or Ishvara desired to become many, and he became the multitude of individual souls and the world. The Jagat or Maya is not false or illusionary, the physical material world is. Vallabha recognises Brahman as the whole and the individual as a ‘part’ (but devoid of bliss) like sparks and fire.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acintya_Bheda_Abheda">Acintya Bheda Abheda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Acintya Bheda Abheda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Achintya_Bheda_Abheda" title="Achintya Bheda Abheda">Achintya Bheda Abheda</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chaitanya_Mahaprabhu" title="Chaitanya Mahaprabhu">Chaitanya Mahaprabhu</a> (1486–1534), stated that the soul or energy of God is both distinct and non-distinct from God, whom he identified as Krishna, <a href="/wiki/Govinda" title="Govinda">Govinda</a>, and that this, although unthinkable, may be experienced through a process of loving devotion (<i><a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">bhakti</a></i>). He followed the Dvaita concept of Madhvacharya.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> This philosophy of "inconceivable oneness and difference".
</p>
<h2><span id="C.C4.81rv.C4.81ka"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cārvāka">Cārvāka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cārvāka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Charvaka</a></div>
<p>The Cārvāka school is one of the <a href="/wiki/%C4%80stika_and_n%C4%81stika" title="Āstika and nāstika">nāstika</a> or "heterodox" philosophies .<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KN_Tiwari_1998_page_67_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KN_Tiwari_1998_page_67-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> It rejects supernaturalism, emphasizes <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">philosophical skepticism</a>, holding empiricism, perception and conditional inference as the proper source of knowledge<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vvraman_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vvraman-149">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Cārvāka is an <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheistic</a> school of thought.<sup id="cite_ref-radhacarvaka_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radhacarvaka-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> It holds that there is neither afterlife nor rebirth, all existence is mere combination of atoms and substances, feelings and mind are an epiphenomenon, and free will exists.<sup id="cite_ref-bhattacarv_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhattacarv-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bronkhorst_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bronkhorst-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bṛhaspati is sometimes referred to as the founder of Cārvāka (also called Lokayata) philosophy. Much of the primary literature of Carvaka, the <a href="/wiki/Barhaspatya_sutras" title="Barhaspatya sutras">Barhaspatya sutras</a> (ca. 600 BCE), however, are missing or lost.<sup id="cite_ref-radhacarvaka_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radhacarvaka-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Its theories and development has been compiled from historic secondary literature such as those found in the <a href="/wiki/Shastra" title="Shastra">shastras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sutra" title="Sutra">sutras</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_epic_poetry" title="Indian epic poetry">Indian epic poetry</a> as well as from the texts of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Jain_literature" title="Jain literature">Jain literature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-radhacarvaka_150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radhacarvaka-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the widely studied principles of Cārvāka philosophy was its rejection of <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inference</a> as a means to establish valid, universal knowledge, and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> truths.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In other words, the Cārvāka epistemology states that whenever one infers a truth from a set of observations or truths, one must acknowledge doubt; inferred knowledge is conditional.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shaivism">Shaivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Shaivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
