<p>Early history of Shaivism is difficult to determine.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Shvetashvatara_Upanishad" title="Shvetashvatara Upanishad"><i><i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">Śvetāśvatara</i> Upanishad</i></a> (400 – 200 BCE)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood199686_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood199686-157">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> is considered to be the earliest textual exposition of a systematic philosophy of Shaivism.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Shaivism is represented by various philosophical schools, including non-dualist (<i>abheda</i>), dualist (<i>bheda</i>), and non-dualist-with-dualist (<i><i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">bhedābheda</i></i>) perspectives. <a href="/wiki/Vidyaranya" title="Vidyaranya">Vidyaranya</a> in his works mentions three major schools of Shaiva thought—<a href="/wiki/Pashupata_Shaivism" title="Pashupata Shaivism">Pashupata Shaivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaiva_Siddhanta" title="Shaiva Siddhanta">Shaiva Siddhanta</a> and Pratyabhijña (<a href="/wiki/Kashmir_Shaivism" title="Kashmir Shaivism">Kashmir Shaivism</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-cowell_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cowell-159">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="P.C4.81.C5.9Bupata_Shaivism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pāśupata_Shaivism">Pāśupata Shaivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Pāśupata Shaivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pāśupata Shaivism (<i>Pāśupata</i>, "of Paśupati") is the oldest of the major Shaiva schools.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> The philosophy of Pashupata sect was systematized by <i><a href="/wiki/Lakulish" class="mw-redirect" title="Lakulish">Lakulish</a></i> in the 2nd century CE. <i>Paśu</i> in <a href="/wiki/Pashupati" title="Pashupati">Paśupati</a> refers to the effect (or created world), the word designates that which is dependent on something ulterior. Whereas, Pati means the cause (or principium), the word designates the Lord, who is the cause of the universe, the pati, or the ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Pashupatas disapproved of Vaishnava theology, known for its doctrine servitude of souls to the Supreme Being, on the grounds that dependence upon anything could not be the means of cessation of pain and other desired ends. They recognised that those depending upon another and longing for independence will not be emancipated because they still depend upon something other than themselves. According to Pāśupatas, soul possesses the attributes of the Supreme Deity when it becomes liberated from the 'germ of every pain'.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Pāśupatas divided the created world into the insentient and the sentient. The insentient was the unconscious and thus dependent on the sentient or conscious. The insentient was further divided into effects and causes. The effects were of ten kinds, the earth, <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">four elements</a> and their qualities, colour etc. The causes were of thirteen kinds, the five organs of cognition, the five organs of action, the three internal organs, intellect, the ego principle and the cognising principle. These insentient causes were held responsible for the illusive identification of Self with non-Self. Salvation in Pāśupata involved the union of the soul with God through the intellect.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shaiva_Siddhanta">Shaiva Siddhanta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Shaiva Siddhanta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Considered normative <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">Tantric</a> Shaivism, Shaiva Siddhanta<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> provides the normative rites, cosmology and theological categories of Tantric Shaivism.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Being a dualistic philosophy, the goal of Shaiva Siddhanta is to become an ontologically distinct Shiva (through Shiva's grace).<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> This tradition later merged with the Tamil Saiva movement and expression of concepts of Shaiva Siddhanta can be seen in the bhakti poetry of the <a href="/wiki/Nayanars" title="Nayanars">Nayanars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996168_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996168-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kashmir_Shaivism">Kashmir Shaivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Kashmir Shaivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Kashmir Shaivism arose during the eighth<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> or ninth century CE<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> and made significant strides, both philosophical and theological, until the end of the twelfth century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> It is categorised by various scholars as <a href="/wiki/Monistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monistic">monistic</a><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Absolute_idealism" title="Absolute idealism">absolute idealism</a>, theistic monism, realistic idealism,<sup id="cite_ref-vibration_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibration-173">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> transcendental physicalism or concrete monism<sup id="cite_ref-vibration_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vibration-173">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>). It is a school of <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aaivism" class="mw-redirect" title="Śaivism">Śaivism</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Trika" class="mw-redirect" title="Trika">Trika</a> and its philosophical articulation Pratyabhijña.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Even though, both Kashmir Shaivism and Advaita Vedanta are non-dual philosophies which give primacy to Universal Consciousness (<i>Chit</i> or <i>Brahman</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> in Kashmir Shavisim, as opposed to Advaita, all things are a manifestation of this Consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> This implies that from the point of view of Kashmir Shavisim, the phenomenal world (<i>Śakti</i>) is real, and it exists and has its being in Consciousness (<i>Chit</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Whereas, Advaita holds that Brahman is inactive (<i>niṣkriya</i>) and the phenomenal world is an illusion (<i>māyā</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The objective of human life, according to Kashmir Shaivism, is to merge in Shiva or Universal Consciousness, or to realize one's already existing identity with Shiva, by means of wisdom, yoga and grace.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_philosophy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">M Chadha (2015), in The Routledge Handbook of Contemporary Philosophy of Religion, states that Vedas were knowledge source but interpreted differently by different schools of Hindu philosophy: "The sacred texts of the Hindus, the Vedas, are variously interpreted by the six traditional Hindu philosophical schools. Even within a single school, philosophers disagree on the import of Vedic statements. (...) Hindu intellectual traditions must be understood as standing for the collection of philosophical views that share a textual connection. There is no single, comprehensive philosophical doctrine shared by all intellectual traditions in Hinduism that distinguishes their view from other Indian religions such as Buddhism or Jainism on issues of epistemology, metaphysics, logic, ethics or cosmology. The Vedas are regarded as Apauruseya, but by the same token, they are not the Word of God either.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Elisa Freschi (2012): The Vedas are not <a href="/wiki/Deontological_ethics" title="Deontological ethics">deontic</a> authorities in absolute sense and may be disobeyed, but are recognized as an <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemic</a> authority by an orthodox school of Hindu philosophy;<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> (Note: This differentiation between epistemic and deontic authority is true for all Indian religions)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For an overview of the six orthodox schools, with detail on the grouping of schools, see: Radhakrishnan and Moore, "Contents", and pp. 453–487.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

Reidel Publishing Company, Dordrecht, Holland, 1988.  <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/90-277-2497-0" title="Special:BookSources/90-277-2497-0">90-277-2497-0</a>.
